        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="60df98080609403a82041749d1cf7f38">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '4a4f5480-af45-435d-8165-edb2944f3af1'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d176215012" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Conventional sodium-based layered oxide cathodes are extremely air sensitive and possess poor electrochemical performance along with safety concerns when operating at high voltage. The polyanion phosphate, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> stands out as an excellent candidate due to its high nominal voltage, ambient air stability, and long cycle life. The caveat is that Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> can only exhibit reversible capacities in the range of 100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 20% below its theoretical capacity. Here, the synthesis and characterizations are reported for the first time of the sodium-rich vanadium oxyfluorophosphate, Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O, a tailored derivative compound of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, with extensive electrochemical and structural analyses. Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O delivers an initial reversible capacity of 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> between 2.5 and 4.5&nbsp;V under the 1C rate at room temperature, with 85% capacity retention after 900 cycles. The cycling stability is further improved when the material is cycled at 50&nbsp;°C within 2.8–4.3&nbsp;V for 100 cycles. When paired with a presodiated hard carbon, Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O cycled with a capacity retention of 85% after 500 cycles. Cosubstitution of the transition metal and fluorine in Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O as well as the sodium-rich structure are the major factors behind the improvement of specific capacity and cycling stability, which paves the way for this cathode in sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs5731-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs5731-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are currently the preferred energy storage technology for most electronic devices and electric vehicles. However, the pressing demand on lithium will likely be a hurdle to support the widespread of LIBs use in electric vehicle, utility grids, and a range of present and future applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0001_R_d176214912e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the development of alternative technologies to LIBs is of utmost significance for the deployment of large scale energy storage technologies. Several studies anticipated that the use of sodium-ion batteries with renewable energies would significantly lower the levelized cost of electricity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0002_R_d176214912e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Indeed, despite the low energy density penalty, sodium-ion batteries are viewed as attractive alternatives because of (1) abundance of sodium, (2) use of less critical components, (3) the ability to leverage and use the manufacturing knowhow of LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0003_R_d176214912e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Power Sources">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>NaVPO<sub>4</sub>F,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0004_R_d176214912e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>O<i><sub>x</sub></i>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0005_R_d176214912e706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. of Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0006_R_d176214912e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Electrochim. Acta ACS Energy Lett. ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Fe(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0007_R_d176214912e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Dalton Trans. Dalton Trans. J. Power Sources Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0008_R_d176214912e751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - RSC Adv. J. Solid State Electrochem.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are example of the most studied sodium-based polyanion cathode materials. To improve the electrochemical performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP), Li et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0009_R_d176214912e765" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared a series of sodium-rich compounds, Na<sub>3+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>M<i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (M = Ni, Mg), by a sol–gel synthesis and examined the impact of Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Mg<sup>2+</sup> substitutions on its crystal structure in a correlation with its electrochemical performance. Bag et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0010_R_d176214912e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> found that the introduction of Mg<sup>2+</sup> into Na<sub>3+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>M<i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (M =nickel, cobalt, magnesium) led to a drastic improvement of the rate performance and cycling of the NVP cathode. Zhang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0011_R_d176214912e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> used a hydrothermal method to synthesize spherical Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.95</sub>Mn<sub>0.05</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>. The researchers suggested that the Mn-doping increased the unit cell volume and facilitated sodium ion transport during the charge and discharge processes. To compensate for the sodium loss during the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), Zhang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0012_R_d176214912e832" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized a sodium-rich and fluorinated phosphate, Na<sub>4</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>, using a mechanical milling of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> and sodium metal. Bianchini et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0013_R_d176214912e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized Na<sub>4</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>FO<sub>2</sub> by the electrochemical insertion of one sodium into Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>FO<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>Our research team developed several advanced sodium-based cathodes such as Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0003_R_d176214912e895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Power Sources">3</a>, <a href="#advs5731-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0006_R_d176214912e895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Electrochim. Acta ACS Energy Lett. ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaFe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (0 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 1),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0014_R_d176214912e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Power Sources J. Power Sources">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2</sub>M<sub>2</sub>Fe(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (M = Co, Ni, Fe),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0007_R_d176214912e931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Dalton Trans. Dalton Trans. J. Power Sources Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>4</sub>Ni<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0008_R_d176214912e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - RSC Adv. J. Solid State Electrochem.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ni<i><sub>y</sub></i>Mn<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>y</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub>..<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0015_R_d176214912e967" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Sustainability Res.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We also synthesized Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0016_R_d176214912e985" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0017_R_d176214912e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ChemSusChem">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> using a hydrothermal-assisted sol–gel method. These materials exhibited outstanding electrochemical performance in half- and full-cell configurations at high temperatures (45 and 55&nbsp;°C). Herein, we report for the first time a hydrothermal synthesis of a new class of sodium-rich cathode material with the formula Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O that could enable the compensation of the irreversible sodium loss in the first charge in a full cell with presodiated hard carbon. The crystal structure was refined by the Rietveld method using high-resolution synchrotron powder diffraction data and was confirmed by high-resolution transmission electron microscopy. The phase stability during cycling was also analyzed using in situ X-ray powder diffraction (XRD). The electrochemical performances were evaluated in sodium half cells at room temperature and at 50&nbsp;°C by galvanostatic cycling, cyclic voltammetry, and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy, whereas the full cells were evaluated at room temperature. The Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O cathode material demonstrates a capacity retention of 85% after 900 cycles in half cell at room temperature. To our knowledge, no similar compounds with other alkali ion have been reported so far in the literature.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs5731-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs5731-sec-0020-title">2 Results and discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs5731-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structure Refinement</h3>
            
            <p>The synthesized cathode material Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O was analyzed using high-resolution synchrotron XRD. The diffractogram of the material recorded in the 2<i>θ</i> range of 1–10 Å (<i>λ</i> = 0.1173 Å) is illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1a</a>. The XRD pattern showed no impurities, and all the peaks are sharp and well-defined, which indicates a high degree of crystallinity of the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O powder.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs5731-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6724546b-3980-4f05-be4c-8caccae551eb/advs5731-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6724546b-3980-4f05-be4c-8caccae551eb/advs5731-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1ced75ff-0fe0-4cbc-a668-eee1c845d466/advs5731-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6724546b-3980-4f05-be4c-8caccae551eb/advs5731-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs5731-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Observed, calculated, and difference plots for X-ray powder diffraction (<i>λ</i> = 0.1173 Å) refinement of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O powder. b) Crystal structure representation of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O, with VO<sub>5</sub>F octahedra and PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra depicted as yellow polyhedral and Na<sup>+</sup> ions represented by black spheres and the schematic of the bioctahedra (V/Ni)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub>F<sub>2</sub>. c) SEM image of the synthesized powder. A high-resolution transmission electron microscopy image of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O along d) [010] and e) [001] zone axis and corresponding Fast Fourier transform (inset of [d]) and (inset of [e]), respectively. Calculated images are shown in the insets and marked by white arrows.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The crystal structure of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O was refined using the structure of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (space group = I4/mmm) as a starting model (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0018_R_d176214912e1185" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At first, a statistical disorder of Ni/V was introduced in the vanadium site, and constraints on their occupancies and atomic displacement parameters (ADPs) were applied. The occupancies of the sodium atoms were then refined, showing a slight increase for Na(1) at the 8&nbsp;<i>h</i> site and no changes for Na(2) content at the 16<i>l</i> site. This led to a composition close to Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>. The presence of vanadyl (V = O) bonds was confirmed by IR absorption spectroscopy. The presence of a vibration mode at ≈911 cm<sup>−1</sup> is in good agreement with previous studies on Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3−2</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0019_R_d176214912e1226" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Solid State Chem. RSC Adv.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, a statistical disorder of F(2)/O(2) was introduced at the F(2) site, and constraints on their occupancies and ADPs were applied. The Rietveld analysis led to the reliability factors listed in <b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#advs5731-tbl-0001">1</a> (<i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 0.0448, <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 0.0605, <i>R</i><sub>B</sub> = 0.0493, and <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> = 3.03) and the composition of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O. The final atomic positions are given in <b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#advs5731-tbl-0002">2</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1a</a> shows an excellent agreement between the experimental and calculated patterns.</p>

                           <td class="center-aligned">4e</td>

                           </tr>

                           <td class="center-aligned">1/4</td>

                           </tr>
                        
                        <tr>

                        <tr>

                           <td class="center-aligned">2a</td>
                           
                           <td class="center-aligned">1</td>

                           </tr>

                           </tr>
                        </tbody>
                     </table>
               </div>
               
               
               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The structure of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O can be described as a 3D network of bioctahedra (V/Ni)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub>F<sub>2</sub> that are connected through the PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1b</a>). The Na<sup>+</sup> cations are in the tunnels running along the three main crystallographic directions, which are [100], [010], and [001]. The (V/Ni)O<sub>5</sub>F octahedra share a common fluorine corner (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1b</a>). The octahedra (V/Ni)O<sub>5</sub>F consists of four equivalent V–O(1) bond distances of 2.007 Å, as well as one short bond distance (V/Ni) = O(2) and one stretched bond distance (V/Ni)–F(1) of 1.683(1) and 2.095(1) Å, respectively. These distances are in good agreement with those observed in the polyanionic compounds Na<sub>3</sub>(VO)Fe(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>VO(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F, and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> with 1.65 and 1.934 Å for the short and stretched distances, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0020_R_d176214912e1820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Solid State Chem. Phys. Rev. B">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra have four P–O(1) bonds with distance of 1.533 Å<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0021_R_d176214912e1828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Mater. Res. Bull. J. Alloys Compd.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is similar to the P–O distances observed in most of the phosphate materials <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0020_R_d176214912e1833" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Solid State Chem. Phys. Rev. B">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides the chemical compositions, the main difference between Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> consists in the shift along the c axis of the V atoms, from the basal plan of the octahedra towards the F2/O2 atoms in order to form the vanadyl (V = O) bond. One should notice that V, F1 and F2/O2 atoms are aligned along the c axis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1b</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs5731-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electron Microscopy Results</h3>
            
            <p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM)&nbsp;image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1c</a>) indicate that Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O has high-quality monodispersed particles with a homogeneous spherical morphology and an average particle size of ≈11&nbsp;µm. Further, the EDS analysis confirms the nominal composition expected of the synthesized materials (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). According to the Na/V ratio from the EDX result, it indicates that Ni2+ goes to a V site and excess Na+ was introduce into the crystal to keep the charge balance. To further confirm the X-ray structural model, high resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) studies were performed along the most informative orientations, which in this case were along [010] and [001] (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1d,e</a>). The composition, determined on several small crystallites by energy-dispersive X-ray analysis in the electron microscope, agrees with the nominal one, except for light elements, although their presence is confirmed. The corresponding HRTEM&nbsp;micrograph (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1d</a>) along [010] shows an apparently well-ordered material with <i>d</i>-spacings of 6.3 and 10.6 Å, corresponding to d<sub>100</sub> and d<sub>001</sub>, respectively. Fast Fourier transform was performed on the HRTEM micrograph (inset images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1d,e</a>), indicating the existence of different domains. However, the whole crystal results were clearly homogeneous, and only the maxima corresponding to the plane (<i>ac</i>) of the centered tetragonal unit cell were observed. The HRTEM image along [001] (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1e</a>) clearly confirms the tetragonal centered unit cell because well-ordered material with <i>d</i>-spacings of 6.3 and 6.3 Å corresponding to d<sub>100</sub> and d<sub>010</sub> are observed. Fast Fourier transform performed on the HRTEM image (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0001">1e</a>) shows that we are dealing with the basal plane (<i>ab</i>) of the tetragonal centered unit cell. Calculated images using a tetragonal centered unit cell fit nicely in this orientation with the experimental image in both thin and thick parts of the crystal. Based on the above results, the information obtained from HRTEM images confirm that the crystal structure of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O corresponds to tetragonal centered unit cell (space group = I4/mmm) as refined by using high-resolution synchrotron XRD.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs5731-sec-0050-title">2.3 NMR Studies</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0060">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="advs5731-sec-0060-title">2.3.1 <sup>19</sup>F NMR</h4>
               
               <p>The NMR measurements indicate that two peaks exist, located at −168 and −203&nbsp;ppm, suggesting fluorine is in two different chemical environments. The <sup>19</sup>F spectrum for Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O is displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> in the Supporting Information. Assuming the crystal structure of the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O is similar to that of pristine Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>, there are two known fluorine sites in the material—F(1) and F(2). The peak at −203&nbsp;ppm corresponds to the fluorine in the F(2) site, owing to bonding with more Na sites than the F(1) sites and shorter bond length.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0018_R_d176214912e1996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0070">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="advs5731-sec-0070-title">2.3.2 <sup>31</sup>P NMR</h4>
               
               <p>The NMR spectra for <sup>31</sup>P in Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> in the Supporting Information. Because of the inclusion of paramagnetic vanadium and nickel, there is considerable anisotropy in the <sup>31</sup>P environment, denoted by the strong presence of spinning sidebands past 20&nbsp;kHz. Three clear peaks are visible at 11, −11, and −156&nbsp;ppm. In addition, there exists a considerably broad region from 1000 to 4000&nbsp;ppm. Because of the limited excitation bandwidth of NMR pulses, this region was detected using a stepwise spin-echo method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0022_R_d176214912e2034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Magn. Res., Ser. A">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This region could be due to the presence of V<sup>4+</sup> oxidation states in the material.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0080">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="advs5731-sec-0080-title">2.3.3 <sup>23</sup>Na NMR</h4>
               
               <p>The sodium NMR of the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O exhibits three peaks, located at 127, 69, and 58&nbsp;ppm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). This is in contrast to previous studies on pristine Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>, which is known to exhibit two major peaks at 92 and 146&nbsp;ppm.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0023_R_d176214912e2078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the two downfield peaks may have shifted upfield due to the inclusion of nickel, the upfield peak at 58&nbsp;ppm does not correspond to any known 23Na peaks in the pristine material. It should be noted that the NMR is much more sensitive than the XRD; thus, the third site may correspond to an amorphous phase that would not be detected by XRD.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0090">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs5731-sec-0090-title">2.4 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs5731-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O for four cycles recorded at 0.05 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage window of 2–4.5&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. The cyclic voltammetry tests indicate the presence of two reversible peaks at 3.6 and 4.1&nbsp;V, which lead to an average operation voltage of 3.85&nbsp;V. The first redox couple corresponding to a V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> redox has anodic and cathodic peaks centered at around 3.7 and 3.6&nbsp;V respectively. This corresponds to the reversible extraction and insertion of one sodium atom into the crystal structure of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O. The second redox couple at 4.0 and 3.9&nbsp;V corresponds to the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> and the reversible intercalation of a second sodium atom. These two redox couples enabled extraction/insertion of two sodium atoms associated with V<sup>3+/</sup>V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> consistent with those reported in the literature for Na<sub>3</sub>(VO)<sub>1.7</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1.3</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>1.6</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1.4</sub>, and Na<sub>3</sub>(VO<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> cathode materials. Additionally, Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O material operates at an average voltage of ≈3.85&nbsp;V, which is higher than those reported for cathodes NaFe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>) (2.8&nbsp;V),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0014_R_d176214912e2206" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Power Sources J. Power Sources">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>1.86</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (3&nbsp;V),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0007_R_d176214912e2220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Dalton Trans. Dalton Trans. J. Power Sources Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (3.2&nbsp;V)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0003_R_d176214912e2232" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Power Sources">3</a>, <a href="#advs5731-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0006_R_d176214912e2232" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Electrochim. Acta ACS Energy Lett. ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs5731-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ecb3a812-f752-4bd9-a456-c5373aef95a9/advs5731-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ecb3a812-f752-4bd9-a456-c5373aef95a9/advs5731-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d2f38954-2a50-408e-a8ba-47e093d6854e/advs5731-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ecb3a812-f752-4bd9-a456-c5373aef95a9/advs5731-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs5731-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cyclic voltammogram curves recorded at room temperature, between 2 and 4.5&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, with a scanning rate of 0.05&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Charge/discharge curves of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O sample, recorded at room temperature, in a CC mode, at 1C rate, in the voltage range 2.5–4.5&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. c) Cycling performance of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O half-cell at 1C recorded room temperature. d) Charge/discharge curves of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O at C/10, C/5, C/3, 1C, 2C, and 5C rates between 2.5 and 4.5&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0002">2b</a> presents the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O in sodium half-cell during the first three cycles. The curves show that the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O| Na half-cell exhibits two potential plateaus at 4.02/4.25 and 3.74/3.96&nbsp;V, which correspond to the redox reactions observed in the cyclic voltammetry measurements. The two plateaus observed in the charge/discharge profile are very similar to those observed in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>1.6</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1.4</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>(VO<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>. The material delivered an initial charge and discharge capacity of 146 and 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> respectively at 1C. The Na half-cell exhibits excellent cycling performance with a stable discharge capacity of 107 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 900 cycles with a capacity retention of about 85% corresponding to high coulombic efficiency of 99.9% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0002">2c</a>). To observe the electrochemical performance of the cathode Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O, a rate capability assessment up to a rate of 5C at room temperature was carried out. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0002">2d</a>, the cell was cycled between 2.5 and 4.5&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na while increasing the C rate from C/10 to 5C in a sodium half-cell. The Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O cathode delivered the discharge capacities of 117, 117, 116, 113, 110, and 105 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the rates of C/10, C/5, C/3, 1C, 2C, and 5C, respectively. This result confirms that the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O cathode exhibits excellent rate capacity thanks to the fast Na<sup>+</sup>-extraction insertion processes into the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O structure at investigated C rates.</p>
            
            <p>To check the possibility of insertion of a fourth Na in Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O framework as reported by Bianchini et&nbsp;al. for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>FO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0013_R_d176214912e2457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the cell was discharged to 1.5&nbsp;V and charged to 4.3&nbsp;V at the rate of 0.1C at 25&nbsp;°C (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs5731-fig-0003">3a</a>). The Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O delivers a charge capacity of 165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which indicates that three sodium atoms have been extracted (theoretical capacity = 65 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for one sodium). However, the capacity started fading rapidly after 45 cycles which could result from side reactions within the wide voltage range (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0003">3b</a>). We further adopted narrower voltage range (2.8–4.3&nbsp;V) for cycling test to improve the capacity retention. Furthermore, we evaluated the charge/discharge performance of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O at 50&nbsp;°C to study the tolerance of the cathode material toward elevated temperature, which is important in preventing thermal runaways that ultimately lead to fire or explosion. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0003">3c</a> presents the first to third galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles for Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> compound at 0.1C and 1C for 50&nbsp;°C using cut-off voltage of 4.3&nbsp;V. The Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O samples showed good long-term durability with the capacity loss of 0.05% per cycle on average at a 1C rate at 50&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0003">3d</a>). It should be noted that for this study we only wanted to detect the impact of potential window on the cycling stability of the developed cathode materials. Further investigation into stabilization of the cathode for the whole voltage window is needed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0024_R_d176214912e2534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Small ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Appl. Surf. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs5731-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/023b20a1-d1fb-4de4-b4db-e05f6ac13185/advs5731-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>

                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Charge/discharge curves of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O cathode, in a CC mode, at 0.1C and 1C rate, in the voltage range 1.5–4.3 and 2.8–4.3&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na recorded at a) 25&nbsp;°C and c) 50&nbsp;°C, respectively.
