         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0100">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs5731-sec-0100-title">2.5 Full-Cell Battery Performance</h3>
            
            <p>For practical application, it is important to demonstrate full cell performance with competitive energy densities. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs5731-fig-0004">4a</a> summarized the nominal voltage and capacities of sodium cathodes that have been reported in literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0025_R_d176214912e2589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ChemElectroChem Phys. Status Solidi Rapid Res. Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Electrochim. Acta Chem. Mater. J. Solid State Electrochem. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Our research team analyzed the performance of the full cell using Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O against presodiated hard carbon anode in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate–dimethyl carbonate electrolyte cycled between 2.8 and 4.2&nbsp;V at 1C rate. The full cell demonstrated excellent cell performance of an initial reversible capacity of 123 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> delivered at 1C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0004">4b</a>). The long-term cycling performance at 1C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0004">4c</a>) indicates that 83&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup> capacity was maintained after 500 cycles, which corresponds to 85% capacity retention. The stable cycling performance can be attributed to sufficient Na<sup>+</sup> in the presodiated hard carbon anode and the excellent cycling performance of the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O cathode material.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs5731-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0d2e32c8-ad00-4bdb-993e-d6a98ca0164d/advs5731-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0d2e32c8-ad00-4bdb-993e-d6a98ca0164d/advs5731-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/be5a8d31-0be5-463c-80f5-8c95db5c9c06/advs5731-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0d2e32c8-ad00-4bdb-993e-d6a98ca0164d/advs5731-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs5731-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Comparison of voltage and specific capacity of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O sodium-ion battery systems that were reported in the literature, with dotted lines indicating energy density that can be achieved at the electrode level against hard carbon anode. b) Charge/discharge curves of the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O | hard carbon full cell and c) its capacity evolution with cycle number at room temperature.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0110">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs5731-sec-0110-title">2.6 In Situ X-Ray Powder Diffraction</h3>
            
            <p>To further confirm the sodium intercalation/deintercalation mechanism into/from the host structure, in situ XRD data were collected during the charge/discharge processes of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O | Na half-cell in the potential range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs5731-fig-0005">5a,b</a>). During the charging process, only the major peak shifting is observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0005">5a</a>). The diffraction peaks (101) and (002) located at 16.16° and 16.66° merged to a single peak and returned to their original position during the discharge, revealing a reversible extraction/insertion of Na ions in the potential range of 2–4.3&nbsp;V. Similar behavior was observed for the peaks (200) and (103) at 27.88° and 28.77° respectively. The peak (002) at 16.66° shifts to a lower 2<i>θ</i> angle, which corresponds to an increase of the <i>c</i> cell parameter. The peak (200) at 27.88° shifts to a higher 2<i>θ</i>, which indicates a decrease of the <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> cell parameters during charging. The reversed situation occurs during discharge, and the complete original pattern is recovered after discharging, indicating the reversible nature of the intercalation process. The cell volume was calculated using the derived lattice parameters (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0005">5c</a>) and decreased from 432.66 to 420.75 Å<sup>3</sup> during the charging process. This agrees with the extraction of sodium ions from the structure and the oxidation of vanadium cations. The XRD operando analysis clearly confirms that during the charging/discharging processes, the sodium intercalation/deintercalation in Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O occurs through a reversible single-phase reaction instead of a two-phase reaction which was observed for many other phosphate-based cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0026_R_d176214912e2744" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result indicates the high structural stability of the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O during electrochemical cycling.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs5731-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5532a8e2-9b43-4d6d-8e05-29d4bd5f1073/advs5731-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5532a8e2-9b43-4d6d-8e05-29d4bd5f1073/advs5731-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ea2ba641-f4b1-4cf4-83f2-73f5296dfceb/advs5731-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5532a8e2-9b43-4d6d-8e05-29d4bd5f1073/advs5731-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs5731-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Operando X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) collected during the first charge/discharge for Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na under a current rate of C/10 between 2 and 4.5 V, and b) corresponding voltage profile. The diffraction results indicate a major peak shifting in situ XRD panels from 15° to 17.5°, 26.9° to 29.9°, and 32.7° to 32.9°. c) Variations of the cell parameters of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O during charge and discharge of the battery are shown.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">The measurements of galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT) and electronic impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were performed as a function of sodium content. The EIS spectra were recorded on the half-cell configuration of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O | Na at different states of charge and discharge. The selected impedance spectra at different states of charge/discharge&nbsp;(SOC/SOD) are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> in the Supporting Information. The cell voltage decay rate was ≈2&nbsp;mV h<sup>−1</sup> at the end of the rest interval under the open circuit voltage (OCV) conditions, ensuring that the partial sodiation and desodiation takes place due to the applied small bias potentials during the EIS measurements. This is because, at steady state OCV condition, the cell voltage of the material lies between the oxidation and reduction states. The measured Nyquist plots of the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O | Na cell indicates the following components corresponding to an equivalent circuit shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> in the Supporting Information

               <ul class="unordered-list">
                  
                  <li>R<sub>1</sub>: An intercept appeared at the real part at a high-frequency interval because of the ionic resistance of the electrolyte solution and a minor contribution of the SEI.</li>
                  
                  <li>R<sub>2</sub>: A first depressed semicircle, at the medium-high frequencies, is due to the charge transfer resistance at the metallic sodium/electrolyte interface and the electronic conductivity of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O.</li>
                  
                  <li>R<sub>3</sub>: A second semicircle appeared at the medium-low frequencies because of the charge transfer reaction at the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O/electrolyte interface, which has higher capacitance value than that of the first semicircle.</li>
                  
                  <li>The Warburg response appeared at the lower frequency interval of the spectrum.</li>
                  </ul>
            </div>
            
            <p>Noting that the similar impedance spectra were appeared at other states of charge and discharge during the measurements process.</p>
            
            <p>The individual cell resistance components were separated by fitting the impedance spectra with the equivalent circuit displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> in the Supporting Information. The ohmic resistance of the electrolyte solution (R<sub>1</sub>) and the interfacial charge transfer resistances (R<sub>2</sub> and R<sub>3</sub> for the two semicircles) are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs5731-fig-0006">6a</a> as a function of the states of charge and discharge. The EIS data comprises of seven circuit elements, which are discussed in detail in the supplementary file (Cf. the equivalent circuit model used to fit the data). It is discernible from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0006">6a</a>, at the beginning of desodiation, the resistance of the first semicircle (R<sub>2</sub>) slightly decreases (up to ≈20% SOC) and, thereafter, remains almost constant with further desodiation. The initial decrease of R<sub>2</sub> is assumed to be due to the change of electronic conductivity of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O on charging. On the other hand, the resistance of the second semicircle (R<sub>3</sub>) initially decreases fast with charging and thereafter, slowly decreases on further desodiation. The resistance of the lower-frequency semicircle (R<sub>3</sub>) is significantly higher than that of the first semicircle (R<sub>2</sub>). In reality, R<sub>2</sub> should not be changed with the changing of SOC as long as it is associated only with the charge transfer reaction at the sodium/electrolyte interface. However, the change of SOC/SOD could generate the mixed valent state of transition metals in the active material and induces the change of electronic conductivity which impacts the charge transfer resistance at the R3/electrolyte interface. However, the R<sub>3</sub> value due to the discharge process is slightly different than the charging process and is not clearly understood. Nonetheless, R<sub>1</sub> and R<sub>2</sub> exhibit similar values and patterns as a function of states of charge and discharge. Recently reported data show that the charge transfer kinetics at the cathode-electrolyte interface can be further enhanced with the increase of electronic conductivity of active materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0027_R_d176214912e2949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Power Sources J. Electrochem. Soc.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It should be noted that the electronic conductivity of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O is relatively low in the fully sodiated phase and increases on partial desodiation. The EIS results indicate that the interfacial charge transfer resistance at the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O | electrolyte interface appears to be rate-limiting.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs5731-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eb405917-e502-4d3f-9ab0-50974090705d/advs5731-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/eb405917-e502-4d3f-9ab0-50974090705d/advs5731-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/455dc7cb-ec23-4b61-bbfe-5e966eb355c3/advs5731-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eb405917-e502-4d3f-9ab0-50974090705d/advs5731-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs5731-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202301091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The ohmic (R1) and electrode/electrolyte interfacial (R2 and R3) resistances, b) fitting of depolarization cell voltage with Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#advs5731-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a> to extract the relaxation time, and c) the sodium-ion diffusivity as a function of state of charge.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">With the Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#advs5731-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>), the relaxation times (<i>τ</i>) was extract by fitting the depolarization cell voltage of the GITT measurements at every 5% states of SOC

               <div class="inline-equation" id="advs5731-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/26cb8422-7e85-4c50-a903-b35c5a9bfbff/advs5731-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs5731:advs5731-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/advs5731-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>ln</mi>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
                              </mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>U</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">t</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">−</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>U</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">t</mi>
                                       <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mo>=</mo>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mi>∞</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
                                 <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                 <mo linebreak="goodbreak">=</mo>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mi>ln</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <mi>A</mi>
                                 <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                              </mrow>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mi>t</mi>
                                 <mi>τ</mi>
                              </mfrac>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\rm{ln\ }}|{U}_{\rm{t}} - {U}_{{\rm{t\ }} = \ \infty }|\ = {\rm{\ ln}}A - \frac{t}{\tau }\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>Details of the equations and data analysis can be found in the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0026_R_d176214912e3060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>&nbsp;Here <i>U</i><sub>t</sub> and <i>U</i><sub>t = ∞</sub> are the cell voltages at times <i>t</i> and <i>t<sub>∞</sub></i>, respectively and <i>A</i> is the constant term for a particular material. Noting that the slope of the plot, ln |<i>U<sub>t</sub></i> − <i>U</i><sub>t = ∞</sub> | versus <i>t</i> provides the relaxation time,&nbsp;<i>τ</i>.</p>
            
            <p>It should be noted that the origin of the exponential type of depolarization of the cell voltage is due to the sodium concentration gradient generated across the active particle during GITT measurements. In fact, the cell voltage was allowed to relax under the OCV condition leading to a steady state at each desodiation/sodiation steps. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0006">6b</a> clearly shows that the depolarization cell voltage is well-fitted with the Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#advs5731-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>). The sodium-ion diffusivity was derived from the obtained relaxation time which is displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0006">6c</a> as a function of the SOC. Noting that 1&nbsp;µm diffusion length was considered based on the SEM microstructure. It should be noted that alternative strategies for estimating ionic diffusivity parameters from GITT measurements exist and the current approach is selected due to need of fewer unknown variables.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0028_R_d176214912e3104" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Electroanal. Chem. J. Electroanal. Chem.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5731-fig-0006">6c</a> shows two trends of ionic diffusivity behavior as a function of the SOC. Initially, the ionic diffusivity gradually decreases upon the removal of sodium up to&nbsp;≈80% SOC and thereafter starts to increase gradually with the further removal of sodium to 100% SOC. Note that the diffusivity value increases to the initial value at the end of SOC. In addition, it should be noted that the diffusion mechanism of cations in crystalline materials takes place through either the interstitial space or vacancy migration, depending on local energy environment. In the interstitial mechanism, the ionic diffusivity decreases as the concentration of mobile ions decreases, whereas in the vacancy-type mechanism, the reverse trend is observed. Therefore, the interstitial mechanism might be active up to&nbsp;≈80% SOC and thereafter change to the vacancy-type mechanism. Nonetheless, the diffusivity values fall between 5×10<sup>−12</sup>&nbsp;and 3×10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> in the whole range of the sodium concentration. The ionic diffusivity can also be a rate-limiting factor at higher cycling rates, and micrometer-size particles would be suitable for practical application.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs5731-sec-0120">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs5731-sec-0120-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully synthesized the sodium rich Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O material by hydrothermal reaction with homogeneous spherical morphology. The impurity-free stable phase is confirmed by XRD and NMR results, which includes the VO<sub>5</sub>F octahedra, PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra, and bi-octahedra (V/Ni)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub>F<sub>2</sub> crystal arrangement. Half cells using Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O as the cathode exhibited an initial capacity of 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C and stable cycling performance performances up to 900 cycles with an 85% capacity retention at room temperature. The higher specific capacity and stability for these materials arises from their sodium-rich structure, as well as co-substitution of transition metal and fluorine in the lattice. In elevated temperature environment (50&nbsp;°C), the half-cell showed improved cycling stability, which addressed safety concerns regarding prevention of thermal runaways. Full cells with Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O cathode and presodiated hard carbon anode achieved an initial reversible capacity of 123 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1C with a capacity retention of 85% after 500 cycles. Na ion diffusivity values upon initial charging/discharging are around 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which indicated fast ionic transport properties in the NASICON-type Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O. Operando XRD results demonstrated reversible phase transition of the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O during charging/discharging. The obtained results indicate that Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O is a promising cathode candidate for practical stable sodium-ion batteries for both high-power and high-energy density applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs5731-sec-0130">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs5731-sec-0130-title">4 Experimental Section</h2> 
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0140">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0150">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs5731-sec-0160-title"> Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O was prepared using a hydrothermal reaction by mixing stoichiometric amounts of NaF (Aldrich, ≥ 99%), NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> (Aldrich, ≥ 99.99%), NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (Aldrich, 99.99%), Ni(CH<sub>3</sub>COO).4H<sub>2</sub>O (Aldrich, ≥ 99%), NaOH (Aldrich, ≥ 99%), and citric acid (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>7</sub>). Here, C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>7</sub> precursor played multiple roles in the synthesis process—chelating agent, reducing agent, and carbon source. First, NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> and citric acid with a mole ratio of 1:2 were dissolved in 40&nbsp;mL of water to form a clear blue solution (solution A), indicating that V<sup>5+</sup> was successfully reduced to V<sup>3+</sup>. Stoichiometric amounts of NaF, NaOH, and NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> were dissolved together in 40&nbsp;mL of H<sub>2</sub>O (solution B). Solution B was then added dropwise to solution A under continuous stirring at 70&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h or until a homogeneous solution was formed. Finally, the solution was poured into a 100&nbsp;mL autoclave, which was heated at 200&nbsp;°C for 20&nbsp;h, then cooled down to room temperature. The resultant solution was ultrasonically treated for 30&nbsp;min to increase the homogeneity of the dispersion and then was heated at 100&nbsp;°C with stirring to evaporate the water. The obtained powder was then sintered at 650&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h under argon.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs5731-sec-0170-title"> X-Ray Powder Diffraction Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>To assess the purity of the Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O sample, X-ray Diffraction (XRD) measurements were performed. The data was collected at room temperature over the 2<i>θ</i>&nbsp;angular range of 10° ≤&nbsp;2<i>θ</i>&nbsp;&nbsp;≤&nbsp;110° with a step size of 0.01° using a Bruker D8 ADVANCE diffractometer operating with Cu-K<i>α</i> radiation. The full pattern-matching refinements were performed using the Jana2006 program package.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs5731-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs5731-bib-0029_R_d176214912e3334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Z. Kristallogr.-Cryst. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The backgrounds were estimated by a Legendre function, and the peak shapes were described by a pseudo-Voigt function. The Rietveld refinements were then performed using high-resolution synchrotron XRD data collected from the 11&nbsp;BM beamline of the Advanced Photon Source (<i>λ</i> = 0.1173 Å).</p>
                  
                  <p>A specific cell with a beryllium window was used for recording operando X-ray diffraction data in reflection mode to study the reaction mechanism of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O active cathode material during electrochemical cycling. The XRD patterns were recorded every hour. Diffractograms were registered during the electrochemical charge/discharge process, using Bruker D8 Discover Twin-Twin with an advanced diffractometer in Bragg–Brentano geometry with Cu-K<i>α</i> radiation (<i>λ</i>&nbsp; = &nbsp;1.540, 60 Å, 15° ≤ 2<i>θ</i> ≤ 40°) and a Bio-Logic VMP3 potentiostat at a rate of C/20.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0180">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs5731-sec-0180-title"> Electron Diffraction Investigations</h4>
                  
                  <p>Selected area electron diffraction and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) were performed in a JEOL 3000 FEG electron microscope, fitted with a double tilting goniometer stage (±22°, ±22°). The local composition was analyzed by energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy with an Oxford INCA analyzer system attached to the above-mentioned microscope. Simulated HRTEM images were calculated by the multislice method using the MacTempas software package.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0190">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs5731-sec-0190-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>Positive electrodes were made from a mixture of Na<sub>3.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>O powder, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride in a weight ratio of 80:10:10. The resulting electrode films were cut into round discs (<i>Φ</i> = 14&nbsp;mm) and dried at 120&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h under vacuum. 100&nbsp;µL of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) (v:v, 1:1) was used as electrolyte. The CR2032 coin cells were assembled against Na metal in an Ar-filled glove box, with the glass fiber (Whatman Grade GF/A, <i>Φ</i> = 20&nbsp;mm) as a separator. Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were performed on an Arbin and Maccor battery testing systems under various current densities. Hard carbon anodes are presodiated in half-cell configuration against Na metal (0.1C for one cycle, then discharged to 100%), washed with PC, and finally dried before further use. Full cells are made by pairing cathodes with presodiated hard carbon anodes with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in PC with 5 vol% fluoroethylene carbonate as the electrolyte. Full cells are cycled between 2.8 and 4.2&nbsp;V at 0.1C (1C = 128&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) for five cycles, then at 1C for extensive cycling.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0200">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs5731-sec-0200-title"> Impedance Spectroscopy Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>In situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed to determine the interfacial charge transfer resistance as a function of sodium content within the cathode. The cell voltage relaxation process was applied to evaluate ionic diffusivity. In the formation cycle, the cell was charged and discharged to form a stable SEI layer, and then a charging/discharging current equivalent to a C/20 rate was applied using a Solartron battery cycler (1470 E). The current was applied for 1&nbsp;h to attain a certain state of charge/discharge. After partial sodiation/desodiation, the cell voltage was relaxed for at least 3&nbsp;h under the open-circuit voltage (OCV) condition to reach the steady state in which the voltage decay is less than 2&nbsp;mV h<sup>−1</sup> at the end of the time interval. Thereafter, EIS measurement was performed in the frequency range of 5&nbsp;MHz to 0.5&nbsp;Hz using a sinusoidal voltage amplitude of 10&nbsp;mV. The measurement procedure was repeated stepwise to cover all states of charge/discharge. The depolarized cell voltage was fitted to evaluate the ionic diffusivity, and the measured EIS spectra were fitted using an equivalent circuit model.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs5731-sec-0210">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs5731-sec-0210-title"> NMR Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>All experiments were performed using a Varian 300&nbsp;MHz Direct Drive Spectrometer with an operating frequency of 301.4&nbsp;MHz at a field of 7.05T. A 3.2&nbsp;mm MAS Chemagnetics broadband probe was utilized, and all samples were packed into 3.2&nbsp;mm thin-walled zirconia rotors with Vespel end caps and drive tips. Single-pulse and Spin Echo experiments were performed to obtain <sup>7</sup>Li, <sup>23</sup>Na, and <sup>31</sup>P spectra at a spinning speed of 19&nbsp;kHz, and <sup>19</sup>F spectra at a speed of 15&nbsp;kHz.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs5731-sec-0220-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
